    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer12870874583743801310">Soren Kierkegaard was a Danish philosopher, theologian, and religious author interested in human psychology. He is regarded as a leading pioneer of existentialism and one of the greatest philosophers of the 19th Century.<br/><br/>In <i>Fear and Trembling</i>, Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when God commanded him to offer his son as a hum</span>
  <span id="freeText12870874583743801310" style="display:none">Soren Kierkegaard was a Danish philosopher, theologian, and religious author interested in human psychology. He is regarded as a leading pioneer of existentialism and one of the greatest philosophers of the 19th Century.<br /><br />In <i>Fear and Trembling</i>, Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when God commanded him to offer his son as a human sacrifice. Abraham had a choice to complete the task or to forget it. He resigned himself to the loss of his son, acting according to his faith. In other words, one must be willing to give up all his or her earthly possessions in infinite resignation and must also be willing to give up whatever it is that he or she loves more than God. Abraham had passed the test -- his love for God proved greater than anything else in him. And because a good and just Creator would not want a father to kill his son, God intervened at the last moment to prevent the sacrifice.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12870874583743801310" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_6201823" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6201823" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/291074-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1190314300p2/291074.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/6201823?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/291074-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">pretentious atheists (i.e., my generation)</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer6201823" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7297913452610950153">dear reader,<br/><br/>you don't even read this stuff anymore, do you?! i wouldn't if i were you! but that's the difference between me and you! you have no life, are pathetic, sit in front of your computer all day stalking your peers on various social networking sites, while i go on constantly mocking your efforts through half jest and utter disregard for the values you hold dear to your heart!<br/><br/>alas, perhaps the joke is on me?!<br/><br/>haha, boy do i get ahead of myself sometimes! silly me! yes, that is what i say! </span>
  <span id="freeText7297913452610950153" style="display:none">dear reader,<br /><br />you don't even read this stuff anymore, do you?! i wouldn't if i were you! but that's the difference between me and you! you have no life, are pathetic, sit in front of your computer all day stalking your peers on various social networking sites, while i go on constantly mocking your efforts through half jest and utter disregard for the values you hold dear to your heart!<br /><br />alas, perhaps the joke is on me?!<br /><br />haha, boy do i get ahead of myself sometimes! silly me! yes, that is what i say! i say, "silly me!" and i sit in the bathtub at night and i make tiny little cuts into the backs of my thighs and the bottom of my feet! the pain let's me know i am alive! anywho! today's book is a classic by the greatly pathetic soren kierkegaard, entitled "fear and trembling: who let the dogs out?" ok let's go!<br /><br /><br />REVIEW:<br /><br />one could easily argue that the central thesis of this book is the idea that "faith begins precisely where reason ends."<br />kierkegaard struggles with faith, simultaneously demonstrating that it is impossible to successfully rationalize faith (i.e., give any kind of logical explanation of it), just as it is impossible to achieve faith by way of reason.<br />another highlight is the four alternative retellings of the story of abraham and isaac, which are truly a mindfuck.<br /><br /><br />VERDICT:<br /><br />in my supremely accurate and overwhelmingly insightful opinion, this book is most important as a device by which to make people at least recognize, and hopefully respect, the great personal struggle and triumph that is religious faith. too many people in this generation, it seems, write religion off without even knowing why they do so, other than that it doesn't agree with what science dictates. kierkegaard here demonstrates the difference and mutual exclusiveness of the two, and thus that it is possible to love and respect both. lol!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7297913452610950153" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText16158966636010356870" style="display:none">Frygt og BÃ¦ven = Fear and Trembling, SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard <br /><br />Fear and Trembling is a philosophical work by SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard, published in 1843 under the pseudonym Johannes de Silentio (John of the Silence). Kierkegaard wanted to understand the anxiety that must have been present in Abraham when "God tested [him] and said to him, take Isaac, your only son, whom you love, and go to the land of Moriah and offer him as a burnt offering on the mountain that I shall show you."<br /><br />Abraham had a choice to complete the task or to refuse to comply to God's orders. He resigned himself to the three-and-a-half-day journey and to the loss of his son. <br /><br />"He said nothing to Sarah, nothing to Eliezer. Who, after all, could understand him, for did not the nature of temptation extract from him a pledge of silence? He split the firewood, he bound Isaac, he lit the fire, he drew the knife." <br /><br />Because he kept everything to himself and chose not to reveal his feelings he "isolated himself as higher than the universal." <br /><br />Kierkegaard envisions two types of people in Fear and Trembling and Repetition. <span id="freeTextContainer9320163055287918368"><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449377015i/17278144._SY540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/><br/>Many readers come to read this book via the Hegel pathway. Or at least realize that a Hegel preamble is required. And most probably such a preamble is indispensable.<br/><br/>Alas, I came to it through a side door. As an attendant of a cycle of lectures given at the <b>Prado Museum </b>on the Bible (Old Testament) and Art, I listened, and looked, in fascination to the exposÃ© of one of the Speakers. He examined the myth of <b>Abraham</b> and the <b>Sacrifice</b> of his beloved son<b> Isaac</b>.<br/><br/>After portraying what he considered an u</span>
  <span id="freeText9320163055287918368" style="display:none"><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1449377015i/17278144._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>Many readers come to read this book via the Hegel pathway. Or at least realize that a Hegel preamble is required. And most probably such a preamble is indispensable.<br><br>Alas, I came to it through a side door. As an attendant of a cycle of lectures given at the <b>Prado Museum </b>on the Bible (Old Testament) and Art, I listened, and looked, in fascination to the exposÃ© of one of the Speakers. He examined the myth of <b>Abraham</b> and the <b>Sacrifice</b> of his beloved son<b> Isaac</b>.<br><br>After portraying what he considered an utterly unethical behavior in the part of Abraham he presented Kierkegaardâs ideas as the only way to approach the dreadful myth. For it cannot be understood.<br><br>For such is the nature of Paradox.<br><br>Abraham was no <b>Agamemnon</b> (*). There was no heroism in his act: Agamemnon was driven by duty; Abraham by faith. Agamemnon could hate his own act but overcome his hatred and announce the intended outcome. Abraham, as the <i>Knight of Faith</i> could not doubt a single instant. He had to want to kill his son, while loving him dearly, because his god had ordered him to do so. And this he had to do quietly.<br><br><blockquote>
  <i>Abraham was greater than all, great by reason of his power whose strength is impotence, great by reason of his wisdom whose secret is foolishness, great by reason of his hope whose form is madness, great by reason of the love which is hatred of oneself.</i>
</blockquote><br><br>And so at the core of Abrahamâs act was the Absurd.<br><br>In this context of absurdity silence, elastic, takes its place. And opens the door to laughter.<br><br>And of the painters,<b> Rembrandt</b>, the master of capturing the interruption, was also the one who represented the force in Abrahamâs unrelenting and unvacillating will. There is no second-guessing god in his Abraham. No acting and no hope. <br><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450003700i/17367925._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>Rembrandt was the one painter who understood what Kierkegaard stated about two hundred years later. Angel had to fight hard to stop Abraham in his unflinching intention to murder.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1450003700i/17367926._SY540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><br>------<br><br>Do I need to point out that Beckett read this book?<br><br>---<br><br>(*) another example of this paternal filicide not mentioned in this book is Emperor Frederick II and his son Henry.</span>

<div id="review_2098716951" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098716951" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/70459127-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666421p2/70459127.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098716951?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/70459127-tara">Tara</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444490" href="/book/show/19742521-fear-and-trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098716951" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4359586071585061797"><blockquote>
  <b>âTo contend with the whole world is a comfort, but to contend with oneself is dreadful.â</b>
</blockquote><br/><i>Fear and Trembling</i> is Kierkegaardâs astonishingly dexterous analysis of faith via the Old Testament story of Abraham and Isaac:<br/><blockquote>
  <i>âAnd it came to pass after these things, that God did tempt Abraham, and said unto himâ¦Take now thy son, thine only son Isaac, whom thou lovest, and get thee into the land of Moriah; and offer him there for a burnt offering upon one of the mountains which I will tell thee of.â </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText4359586071585061797" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <b>âTo contend with the whole world is a comfort, but to contend with oneself is dreadful.â</b>
</blockquote><br><i>Fear and Trembling</i> is Kierkegaardâs astonishingly dexterous analysis of faith via the Old Testament story of Abraham and Isaac:<br><blockquote>
  <i>âAnd it came to pass after these things, that God did tempt Abraham, and said unto himâ¦Take now thy son, thine only son Isaac, whom thou lovest, and get thee into the land of Moriah; and offer him there for a burnt offering upon one of the mountains which I will tell thee of.â Â  Â  Â  âGenesis 22:1</i>
</blockquote> <br>According to Kierkegaard, Abraham was a true âknight of faith.â He didnât merely resign himself to losing his son, but instead believed that Isaac wouldnât actually be harmed. He had faith based on âthe strength of the absurd,â or in spite of the fact that it made no rational sense to do so. Kierkegaard contended that Abrahamâs belief in this undeniable absurdity elevated him to the highest plane of faith one can possibly hope to attain.<br><br>Kierkegaard then made an apparently simple (yet really rather profound) point: Abrahamâs decision to make a leap of faith could not concern itself with the OUTCOME of that leap:<br><blockquote>
  <i>âSurely anyone with a speck of <i>erectior ingenii</i> [nobility of mind] cannot become so completely the cold and clammy mollusc as to lose sight altogether, in approaching the great, of the fact that ever since the Creation it has been accepted practice for the outcome to come last, and that if one is really to learn something from the great it is precisely the beginning one must attend to. <b>If anyone on the verge of action should judge himself according to the outcome, he would never begin.</b> Even though the result may gladden the whole world, that cannot help the hero; for he knows the result only when the whole thing is over, and that is not how he becomes a hero, but by virtue of the fact that he began.â</i>
</blockquote><br>Kierkegaard concluded this section of the book by stating that faith is fundamentally a paradox, and that it goes beyond what reason can comprehend. He then proceeded to examine three âproblemata.â <br><br><b>Problema 1:</b> <i>âIs there a teleological suspension of the ethical?â</i> Kierkegaard answered in the affirmative. Basically, taking care of your fellow human beings is generally your most important ethical concern. However, since murdering a helpless <i>child</i> doesnât usually provide much benefit to humanity, and yet God commanded Abraham to do so, ethical considerations must have been suspended in favor of a higher imperative.<br><br><b>Problema 2:</b> <i>âIs there an absolute duty to God?â</i> Short answer: Yes. Slightly longer explanation: Kierkegaard held that <i>âfaithâs paradox is this, that the single individual is higher than [the human race], that the single individual determines his relation to [the human race] through his relation to [God].â</i> I tinkered with the previous quote in an effort to make it more user friendly. Kierkegaard was kind of a dweeb with some of his jargon and specialized slang. Perhaps also with his coiffure:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1505075451i/23879298.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><b>Problema 3:</b> <i>âWas it ethically defensible of Abraham to conceal his purpose from Sarah, from Eleazar, from Isaac?â</i> Kierkegaard was of the opinion that honesty is usually the best policy. In Abrahamâs case, however, since his task was Absurd with a capital A, and as such could not possibly be understood, he had to grapple with it alone. Abraham couldnât even attempt to relate it to anyone else, since trying to explain what is absurd and incomprehensible isâ¦wellâ¦absurd.<br><br>Kierkegaard wrapped things up by asserting that <i>âthe highest passion in a human being is faith,â</i> and that this is something each person must wrestle with and ultimately earn for themselves.<br><br>Thus concludes my (probably overly detailed) summary of the main points presented in the book. Iâd now like to share a few of Kierkegaardâs more brilliant quotes:<br><blockquote>
  <i>âSomeone who has understood lifeâs horror has grasped Daubâs meaning when he says that a soldier standing guard alone with a loaded gun by a powder magazine on a stormy night gets strange thoughts.â<br><br>âThere is greatness in meriting the tears of those who deserve to shed them; great indeed for the poet to dare hold the crowd in check, dare discipline people into testing their own worthiness to weep for the hero, for the waste-water of snivellers is a degradation of the holy.â<br><br>âAesthetics is the most faithless of all sciences. Anyone who has truly loved it will in a way become unhappy; while anyone who has never done so is and will remain a blockhead.â</i>
</blockquote><br>Overall, Kierkegaard was a phenomenal writer with an often merciless wit. His mind was arrestingly complex, nimble, fluid. I canât claim to fully grasp everything he wrote; at times I got tangled up in the threads of his subtly nuanced logic, especially when I was frantically trying to keep up with some of his more peculiar terminology. That said, many of his points were beautifully reasoned, some even striking in their clarity. I especially appreciated his musings on the suffering that standing alone with your beliefs necessarily entails, and the importance of not letting the pain erode your resolve. <br><br>Although the aforementioned merits made this a fascinating read, I couldnât wholly relate to it, as I do not count religious faith among the more worthwhile pursuits available to us as thinking beings. I <i>do</i> agree with him on this:<br><blockquote>
  <i>âHow monstrous a paradox faith is, a paradox capable of making a murder into a holy act well pleasing to God, a paradox which gives Isaac back to Abraham, which no thought can grasp because faith begins precisely where thinking leaves off.â</i>
</blockquote><br>I just tend to side with Hitchens as to its rather dubious value:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1504379545i/23751430._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="4359586071585061797" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2098219931" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2098219931" />
    <a title="Steven Godin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin"><img alt="Steven Godin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605181037p2/26706841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2098219931?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444490" href="/book/show/19742521-fear-and-trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=essays">essays</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=scandinavia-iceland">scandinavia-iceland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2098219931" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15737608721915428095">It is not an exaggeration to say that Fear and Trembling (1843) was a challenging piece for me to to read, maybe being someone of no religious faith had something to do with it. Kierkegaard (Johannes de silentio) compounds the essential difficulty that lies within the theme of the work, the Akedah, through choosing an alternative pseudonym to praise Abraham as a knight of faith and examine his movements. That the pseudonym's perspective is shrouded in silence seemingly precludes any clear and st</span>
  <span id="freeText15737608721915428095" style="display:none">It is not an exaggeration to say that Fear and Trembling (1843) was a challenging piece for me to to read, maybe being someone of no religious faith had something to do with it. Kierkegaard (Johannes de silentio) compounds the essential difficulty that lies within the theme of the work, the Akedah, through choosing an alternative pseudonym to praise Abraham as a knight of faith and examine his movements. That the pseudonym's perspective is shrouded in silence seemingly precludes any clear and straightforward understanding of this work. Ultimately, whether Kierkegaard's Johannes de silentio is to be read with irony or edification appears as undecidable as whether we should view Abraham as a murderous madman -- who in contrast to Nietzsche's madman proclaiming the death of god proclaims a living god who has commanded the death of his son and then later a ram, or the great father of faith. <br /><br />He goes over the story of Abraham and Isaac and can make no sense of it. he concludes that 'faith' must be a 'leap in the dark' Take the leap he seems to say and God will catch you. Most people do no such thing. They are too sensible and do not jump anywhere unless there is a soft landing of a safety net. Sadly he has bequeathed to the world the idea that Christianity is a religion and belief in god is not rational. Generations of Humanists, Rationalists and Materialists have taken this up as a stick with which to beat Christians and Christian belief. Because he thought God told him to?. Will this have you either going to church every Sunday believing it's OK to kill your kids as long as God gives you his blessing, or tearing the pages out and throwing the cover across the room screaming and swearing at Kiekegaard for being morally and ethically wrong. Like I said as piece of historical philosophy it challenged me, something most books I read never do, hence the four stars. But still left me feeling bemused and dumbfound. Definitely requires a second reading, but me doubt will happen. It's the sort of book that could fire great debate and war of words. I will just sit on the fence though and keep my opinions to myself.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15737608721915428095" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_192662547" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/192662547" />
    <a title="Jan-Maat" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat"><img alt="Jan-Maat" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407579968p2/5617661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/192662547?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jan-Maat" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jan-Maat" href="/user/show/5617661-jan-maat">Jan-Maat</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0140444491" href="/book/show/332594.Fear_and_Trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=scandinavia">scandinavia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=thinking">thinking</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=19th-century">19th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5617661-jan-maat?shelf=read-in-translation">read-in-translation</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer192662547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14871965098921304405">It seems to me that after reading "Fear and Trembling" that all of my thinking on faith lies within Kierkegaard. Which isn't to claim that I understand his arguments but that his arguments have come to dominate the way I think about the issues.<br/><br/>Curiously although Kierkegaard's voice comes at us from the margins he seems oddly part of a broad current of nineteenth century writing, Dostoevsky, if he cold have got past the author being a non-Russian and a Lutheran would have agreed with the emphasis</span>
  <span id="freeText14871965098921304405" style="display:none">It seems to me that after reading "Fear and Trembling" that all of my thinking on faith lies within Kierkegaard. Which isn't to claim that I understand his arguments but that his arguments have come to dominate the way I think about the issues.<br /><br />Curiously although Kierkegaard's voice comes at us from the margins he seems oddly part of a broad current of nineteenth century writing, Dostoevsky, if he cold have got past the author being a non-Russian and a Lutheran would have agreed with the emphasis on faith alone I feel. Though then again I can be no adequate reader of Kierkegaard as he reveals himself only through a nest of alternative identities as though engaging in plausible deniability, or hide and seek, with the reader.<br /><br />I think I read this first, and then was brought back to it several times by reading Dostoevsky more seriously in my 20s and then by means of David Lodge's novel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/20782778.Therapy__Therapy__1_" title="Therapy (Therapy #1) by Kathryn Perez" rel="nofollow noopener">Therapy</a> - although Kierkegaard is more of a staging post in his downward path until the central character clings to a desperate ridiculous, plan <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ returning to his long lost first girlfriend, thirty? Forty? years after separating <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> through faith alone which results in his renewal.<br /><br />Historically I think it's interesting because it offers in the knight of faith a rejection of the triumph of reason. Not having been raised as regular church goer, I was slightly surprised by Kierkegaard, because my assumption was that all religious people were naturally Kieregaardian knights of faith.<br /><br />What strikes me as interesting about the Abraham story is that he isn't bothered by the concept of child sacrifice, which seems to be as a concept an entirely reasonable one to him, what is bvothersome is just the logical conundrum of how God may not be sticking to his side of the bargain and that leads, even requires Kierkegaard to dub Abraham a knight of faith, the champion of <i>sola fide</i>. We might well think that in those days God was plainly so slippery and elusive that one was obliged to cling to pure faith to avoid being completely hopeless. A golden calf is at least reliably golden and immobile.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14871965098921304405" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Greg Watson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Greg Watson" href="/user/show/86305081-greg-watson">Greg Watson</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2525694929" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1786441731088297066">Fear and Trembling is an in-depth and challenging look at the Old Testament account of Abraham's willingness to sacrifice Isaac to God. The story is familiar, perhaps all too familiar as Kierkegaard notes. Without thinking, we jump to the outcome of the story or pass it off as a "trial," forgetting the time leading up to Abraham's testing in which he was silent. In his obedience to God, Abraham showed the paradox of faith -- he loved Isaac enough to be willing to lose him, but he had sufficient </span>
  <span id="freeText1786441731088297066" style="display:none">Fear and Trembling is an in-depth and challenging look at the Old Testament account of Abraham's willingness to sacrifice Isaac to God. The story is familiar, perhaps all too familiar as Kierkegaard notes. Without thinking, we jump to the outcome of the story or pass it off as a "trial," forgetting the time leading up to Abraham's testing in which he was silent. In his obedience to God, Abraham showed the paradox of faith -- he loved Isaac enough to be willing to lose him, but he had sufficient faith to believe that God would not require this of him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1786441731088297066" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/19028555-fear-and-trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/793473-clif-hostetler?shelf=religion">religion</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2155727639" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer483879111442804943"><i>Fear and Trembling</i> was originally published in 1843 written in Danish and under a pseudonymous name. The purpose of the book was two fold. First Kierkegaard wanted to describe the nature of true faith using the story of <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham' rel='nofollow noopener'>Abraham</a> almost sacrificing <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac' rel='nofollow noopener'>Isaac</a> to illustrate the concept. Second he wanted to counter the philosophy of <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel' rel='nofollow noopener'>Hegel</a> who maintained that reason was the highest form of thought. Kierkegaard argued that faith was higher than reason.<br/><br/>However, Kierkegaard's understanding of faith was somet</span>
  <span id="freeText483879111442804943" style="display:none"><i>Fear and Trembling</i> was originally published in 1843 written in Danish and under a pseudonymous name. The purpose of the book was two fold. First Kierkegaard wanted to describe the nature of true faith using the story of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abraham" rel="nofollow noopener">Abraham</a> almost sacrificing <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isaac" rel="nofollow noopener">Isaac</a> to illustrate the concept. Second he wanted to counter the philosophy of <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georg_Wilhelm_Friedrich_Hegel" rel="nofollow noopener">Hegel</a> who maintained that reason was the highest form of thought. Kierkegaard argued that faith was higher than reason.<br /><br />However, Kierkegaard's understanding of faith was something different or beyond common understandings of the word in everyday usage. To distinguish the faith he's talking about Kierkeaard uses the term Knight of Faith. According to him anyone who says they are a Knight of Faith is by definition not a Knight of Faith. It is a personal characteristic that can't be shared. <br /><br />For that matter it can't be explained, understandable, or made rational. Nevertheless, this book attempts to do just that. <br /><br />My main problem with the book is that the story of nearly sacrificing a son as told in the story of Abraham and Isaac is abhorrent to my senses. I would much rather have faith explained using some other story. Ironically, the story of a father killing their child for supposedly honorable reasons seems to have been a fairly popular plot line in ancient literature. The two prominent examples noted in this book are the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iphigenia" rel="nofollow noopener">Agamemnon/Iphigenia</a> and the <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jephthah" rel="nofollow noopener">Jephthah/daughter</a> stories. These as well as the Abraham story had their origins in the Bronze Age and were probably passed along in the oral tradition many years before they were written down. I can see how a story like this would grab the attention of the listeners.<br /><br />Kierkegaard maintains that Abraham is a true Knight of Faith because he acted only in response to God's request and his planned action was known only to himself (and God). Agamemmon's and Jephthah's actions on the other hand were public and done to maintain personal honor, thus they are not true Knights of Faith.<br /><br />I'm inclined to believe that Hegel's philosophy makes more sense than Kierkegaard until it's pointed out that the Nazis and Communists used Hegel to prove that loyalty to the government was the highest calling. By contrast Kierkegaard's message places responsibility of one's action on the individual. Viewed that way Kierkegaard makes more sense. Although, the fact that I say Kierkegaard makes sense is an indication that I don't understand him properly because he says faith doesn't make sense.<br /><br />I would never read <i>Fear and Trembling</i> on my own initiative. It was discussed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/group/show/9977-great-books-kansas-city" rel="nofollow noopener">Great Books KC</a> group of which I am a part. Fortunately, members of the group are smarter than me so the discussion went well.</span>
  <a data-text-id="483879111442804943" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2276344513" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2276344513" />
    <a title="Khush" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76759105-khush"><img alt="Khush" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2276344513?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khush" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khush" href="/user/show/76759105-khush">Khush</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2276344513" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11803198432196433569"><br/><br/><br/><br/>I read this book in translation. I was in awe of its author. However, the book is an easy read, and the central situation (that Abraham has to sacrifice his only son Issac on God's command) around which the whole text revolves is intriguing and exciting too. Almost on every second page, I would read a line or two, and then reflect on what is relayed. For instance, ''Faith begins where reason stops,'' and there are long sentences that one can think about for a long time. It is one of those books </span>
  <span id="freeText11803198432196433569" style="display:none"><br /><br /><br /><br />I read this book in translation. I was in awe of its author. However, the book is an easy read, and the central situation (that Abraham has to sacrifice his only son Issac on God's command) around which the whole text revolves is intriguing and exciting too. Almost on every second page, I would read a line or two, and then reflect on what is relayed. For instance, ''Faith begins where reason stops,'' and there are long sentences that one can think about for a long time. It is one of those books that has to be read slowly. Look at this; ''If there was no eternal consciousness in man, and if at the bottom of everything there was only a wildly seething power â¦ if beneath everything there lurked a bottomless void never to be filledâ what else were life but despair! If it were thus, and if there were no sacred bonds which knit mankind together, if one generation followed upon another like leaves in the forest â¦ how hollow and without consolation life would be.'' The book has many such amazing passages.<br /><br />I also found the 'Notes' at the end of the book interesting. Kierkegaard did not write this book in his own name, but he was superbly conscious of its depth and worth. In fact, he was quite cocky about his achievement. In his journals, he wrote that this book alone has the strength to immortalize him. It is rare that someone knows his writing so well ( the content and value of his work) and can issue such definitive statements. I admire him because all such claims finally became true, and he knew this all along. In the contemporary world, especially in the western world, we talk a lot about 'culture,' 'clash of civilizations', religionsâ particularly Islam. The West also has a very settled tendency to separate itself from the rest, and views its tradition and culture in strictly demarcated boundaries of East/West (so famously espoused by Kipling). However, there is a lot in the Danish philosopher that blurs these rigid binaries. One can trace several key concepts of Eastern Philosophy in his works, particularly Hinduism. The books dwells a lot on the idea of faith and what it means. The kind of significance that Kierkegaard assigns to 'Faith' somehow directs one's attention to the various tropes of Islam in the western imagination.Â Of course, there is much in the book that is singularly Kierkegaard's âhis contribution to the world.</span>

<div id="review_754669113" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/754669113" />
    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4055243-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1455356829p2/4055243.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/18713086-fear-and-trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4055243-michael?shelf=more-than-meets-the-eye">more-than-meets-the-eye</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4055243-michael?shelf=quit-school-and-read">quit-school-and-read</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer754669113" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13035005131627696727">Faith, is âthe paradox of existence.â It is âlook[ing] impossibility in the eye.â <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1384438767i/6876670.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/>As such, Faith is the subject of this work which Kierkegaard limns through the pseudonymous authorshipâa stratagem that I think grants him some of the freedom reserved for the writer of fictionâof one Johannes de silentio. So who is Johannes de silentio? <br/><br/>He is funny:<br/><br/>	âHere we already have plenty to speak of for several Sundays, so there is no need to rush.â<br/><br/>down-to-earth(?):<br/><br/>	âInstead of learning from this that he (t</span>
  <span id="freeText13035005131627696727" style="display:none">Faith, is âthe paradox of existence.â It is âlook[ing] impossibility in the eye.â <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1384438767i/6876670.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br>As such, Faith is the subject of this work which Kierkegaard limns through the pseudonymous authorshipâa stratagem that I think grants him some of the freedom reserved for the writer of fictionâof one Johannes de silentio. So who is Johannes de silentio? <br><br>He is funny:<br><br>	âHere we already have plenty to speak of for several Sundays, so there is no need to rush.â<br><br>down-to-earth(?):<br><br>	âInstead of learning from this that he (the puny sectarian) is incapable of greatness and plainly admitting it, something I cannot but approve since it is what I myself doâ¦â<br><br>and snarky: <br><br>	âI for my part have devoted considerable time to understanding the Hegelian philosophy, believe also that I have more or less understood it, am rash enough to believe that at those points where, despite the trouble taken, I cannot understand it, the reason is that Hegel himself hasnât been altogether clear.â<br><br>One might even say there is a bit humility in his admitting his rashness; and further, that in this humility, he will allow his readers to make similarly ârashâ claims:<br><br>I for my part have devoted not all that much but some time to understanding the Kierkegaardian philosophy, believe also that I have more or less understood it, am rash enough to believe that I can underhandedly imply that I am being humble by likewise admitting my rashness after having just invoked âa bitâ of humility in Johannes de silentioâs admission, when I too claim that [I] am rash enough to believe that at those points where, despite the trouble taken, I cannot understand it, the reason is that Johannes de silentio himself hasnât been altogether clear. Perhaps one could fairly say such a thing about almost any philosopher worth talking about. To Kierkegaardâs credit, I donât think he is unaware of this. It (the understanding and communication of the abstract) is in fact the problem of human existence. <br><br>In Fear and Trembling Johannes de silentio takes the liberty of making a very specific interpretation of the Abraham/Isaac story in order to convey his points. He wants us to believe that, for Abraham, it was the ultimate difficulty, not to nearly murder his son, but to believe, as he is going about the business of it, in the absurdity that he will do this oft frowned-upon deed of fatally knifing oneâs own offspring and yet not lose the child. Okay? Now it seems easy enough to go along with Johannes when he says that the Knight of Faith, by clinging to what he is to sacrifice while yet performing it, is proving himself the doer of a more difficult and significant act than the Knight of Resignation, who gives up his attachment in order to perform the same act. And it also seems rather easy to think that for me or you believing in this absurdity may equate to some sort of ultimate act i.e. difficulty.<br><br>But what I am suggesting is not âaltogether clearâ in this work is the reason that this act is being deemed so difficult for Abraham. Primarily because Abraham, presumably unlike most everyone else, had been in a somewhat regular and relatively direct communion with God prior to his ultimate trial; and this, to my thinking, would certainly skew the threshold of what is thought to be human understanding. One might even claim that Johannes de silentio doesnât even gloss over this fact. For Abraham, who has had many a talkin-to by the Godâit turns out God literally put the âhaâ in Abraham; it being Abram prior to this little ironyâcan it really be that difficult to accept any kind of absurdity? My human understanding is based, at least partially, on the fact that God has been absent from my experience in a way that âheâ was not in Abrahamâs. Long before Abraham raised is recently sharpened sacrificin-blade, his understanding, if it still could be called human understanding, had been obscured by his contact with the Infinite; and so, I tend to think, he is much better equipped to perform in paradox, and his performance isnât quite the ultimate act of faith that Johannes de silentio wants it to be.<br><br>That said, I do think Mr. de silentio or should I say Kierkegaard is doing something important here. He is seizing from our reason a conceptâin this case âFaithââand flinging it into the distance, so that it is strewn far from the feeble reach of our imagination (one wonders if perhaps Kierkegaard heard Depeche Modeâs âPersonal Jesusâ ââreach out and touch faithââ one too many times that day), so that, if only for a moment, we stand humbled and awed by the limits, not of our current understanding, but by the limits of understanding itself. Humbled because it defines the human condition; awed because it points toward the Infinite. And this move, I think, is something that is done far too seldom in our attempts to understand the world, be it through philosophy, religion, science etc. By his, perhaps retrofitted, perhaps retro-omitted, retelling of the Abraham/Isaac story, he is recognizing the absurdity of the human condition. And by our listening to this retelling, along with a bit of Kierkegaard biography we can see a sort of double allegory of this, our human condition:<br><br>A translatorâs note tells us that Kierkegaard wrote in his journal the same year that Fear and Trembling came on the scene: âIf I had faith I would have stayed with Regine.â Regine, it seems, is Kierkegaardâs Isaac from whom he broke off his marriage, presumably for ambitions higher than her petty finiteness. Is it possible that Kierkegaardâs regret informsâor, if you will, corruptsâJohannes de silentioâs faith that having Faith will reward one with oneâs finite desires as well as reconciling one with the Infinite? As if unable to escape the torture of what heâd given up, he makes Faith an impossible move wherein it is possible to have oneâs cake and eat it too. But then, who among us is free of doing such things?<br><br>Despite said contentions, despite this being my first foray into the Kierkegaardian Canon, I have reason to believe that SÃ¸ren is not so susceptible to such flaws in other works. In my quick search for an image, I came across so many superlative quotes that Iâve realized Iâve been going along with a Kierkegaardian shaped hole in my life; so now illumed, Iâm determined to fill the void. And for that, one of those quotes to hold me (and perhaps you) over:<br><br>	âA fire broke out backstage in a theater. The clown came out to warn the public; they thought it was a joke and applauded. He repeated it; the acclaim was even greater. I think thatâs just how the world will come to an end: to general applause from wits who believe itâs a joke.â<br><br>To me, being this serious and this funny at the same time is the mark of brilliance.</span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1228855538" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer6772585459514126961">I'll wholeheartedly admit that I don't completely understand everything in these kinds of philosophy books as I'm not a Kierkegaard scholar and I'm certainly not a philosophy scholar, but I do understand his messages, his profound messages hidden in a cobweb of philosophical jargon, Christian study, Greek mythology references, European fairy tales and some poetry sprinkled on top of it all.<br/><br/><i>Fear and Trembling</i> is his investigation into the paradox of faith, his complex analysis of Christianity and</span>
  <span id="freeText6772585459514126961" style="display:none">I'll wholeheartedly admit that I don't completely understand everything in these kinds of philosophy books as I'm not a Kierkegaard scholar and I'm certainly not a philosophy scholar, but I do understand his messages, his profound messages hidden in a cobweb of philosophical jargon, Christian study, Greek mythology references, European fairy tales and some poetry sprinkled on top of it all.<br /><br /><i>Fear and Trembling</i> is his investigation into the paradox of faith, his complex analysis of Christianity and of human emotions. In this book, Kierkegaard looks into the anxiety of Abraham and the sacrifice of his son Isaac, his complete devotion to God and its dangerous paradoxical reality. His meditations on the individual self, infinite resignation and the knight of faith are sublime examples of existential thought, acute psychological insight and keen awareness of the human condition.<br /><br />What makes Kierkegaard different from other philosophers, however, is his wit, his irony, his brilliant sense of humour and poetic prose, which makes <i>Fear and Trembling</i> an immeasurably great work of philosophy as well as literature at its purest.<br /><br />(Brief thought: What if SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard wrote novels? <i>Hmm, interesting...</i>) </span>
  <a data-text-id="6772585459514126961" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_15672827" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/15672827" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/54697-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1353271036p2/54697.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/15672827?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/54697-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 0140444491" href="/book/show/332594.Fear_and_Trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=top-shelf">top-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=existentialism">existentialism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=wisdom-philosophical-investigatons">wisdom-philosophical-investigatons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/54697-matt?shelf=shattering">shattering</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer15672827&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer15672827" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7987290335343016678">One of the most powerful examinatons of religious faith I have ever read. <br/><br/>It is written deeply, exactingly, disturbingly. If you take it for what it is, it will give you an emotional tumble, a mental workout, and a spiritual shake by the shoulders.<br/><br/>This is the kind of thing they keep a thousand miles from Sunday school. The real, raw, fucked-up aspects of believing in the Big Man Upstairs. This is one of the most influential Christian texts one can find in the modern era, and I originally had tha</span>
  <span id="freeText7987290335343016678" style="display:none">One of the most powerful examinatons of religious faith I have ever read. <br /><br />It is written deeply, exactingly, disturbingly. If you take it for what it is, it will give you an emotional tumble, a mental workout, and a spiritual shake by the shoulders.<br /><br />This is the kind of thing they keep a thousand miles from Sunday school. The real, raw, fucked-up aspects of believing in the Big Man Upstairs. This is one of the most influential Christian texts one can find in the modern era, and I originally had that word in quotes because I mean 90% of the books that use the term wouldn't have any idea how to stand up to its poetic, rigorous, existential theological onslaught.<br /><br />I mean, I reject Christianity, but this I respect even if I humbly disavow some of the theological points it necessarily takes from the Bible (and for granted, at that!).<br /><br />Kierkegaard wrote it from the perspective of the unbeliever, one of his many fictional counterparts, and this narrator is astonished by what he is describing, even as he describes it.<br /><br />It should go without saying that what is promulgated nowadays through Christian media etc. is animal crackers and cheeze wizz. <br /><br />This is thunder and roiling oceans, way beyond fire and brimstone, into real horror and logical explosion.<br /><br />The analytical, dialectical examination of the entire situation will knock you on your ass. I've read the damn thing five or six times (senior thesis in Philosophy) and it got to the point where I actually felt like I was (forgive me) Communicating with Kierkegaard, having gone over the arguments and the language again and again. Even then, it threw me for a loop....<br /><br />"Fear And Trembling" is the moment when Abraham brings Isaac to the mountain, having been called upon to sacrifice him by God. He doesn't want to do it, he's horrified to do it, but do it he must. It can't be explained, reasoned away, or retroactively justified, and anyone who tries to is missing the point entirely. <br /><br />It can't be explained, it can only be Represented. Marveled at, as Kierkegaard does. <br /><br />This is one of the few representations of that existential moment, also known as the Absurd- and the enormous, terrifying leap of faith that it requires- you will ever find.<br /><br />"The teleological suspension of the ethical"- aka "morality don't mean shit when it comes to matters of faith" is a big, sticky, problem. We see it all the time in the news, and the nightmare logic touches on church/state, terrorism, visions, war, love, death....all the most important and provocative (in the literal sense of the word) issues of our time. These issues, of course, never EVER go away...there are so many uncomfortable parts of the fucked-up human psyche that this touches on that it seems like human beings can't overcome the Absurd even in what they think of as their deepest and most authentic actions. <br /><br />Nietzsche (whom I always suspected as having more in common with Kierkegaard than is usually imagined) once wrote that whatever occurs outside of love occurs beyond good and evil. <br /><br />That's a little taste of the kind of thing you're in for if you take on this story/tract/mediation/manifesto/prose poem.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7987290335343016678" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_22717739" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/22717739" />
    <a title="Cap" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1047158-cap"><img alt="Cap" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/22717739?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cap" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cap" href="/user/show/1047158-cap">Cap</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22717739" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3370006430957551260">I first read this piece in a philosophy class devoted entirely to Kierkegaard. At the time I wasn't overly enthralled with his work. I think I was partly turned off by the know it all sophomore in the graduate level class who insisted on being smack dab in the middle and dominating every conversation. <br/><br/>In the years since, however, after reading other existentialist authors, and seeing K's influence on them, I've gone back to some of his more accessible works. I especially like that most of his ph</span>
  <span id="freeText3370006430957551260" style="display:none">I first read this piece in a philosophy class devoted entirely to Kierkegaard. At the time I wasn't overly enthralled with his work. I think I was partly turned off by the know it all sophomore in the graduate level class who insisted on being smack dab in the middle and dominating every conversation. <br /><br />In the years since, however, after reading other existentialist authors, and seeing K's influence on them, I've gone back to some of his more accessible works. I especially like that most of his philosophy is based in Christianity and biblical passages. The upshot is that you don't need to know generations of philosophers that came before in order to understand what's going on, as long as you've got some background in scripture. I guess the other side of that coin is that if you don't, it seems like he's talking about the same thing for hundreds of pages instead of myriad facets of that thing. <br /><br />Fear and Trembling, in the end, is about faith. Not just belief, but what Kierkegaard saw as the foundation of a religious life. The belief that comes despite all indications to the contrary. In opening up his lecture, he starts with several versions of the way the Abraham and Isaac story could have gone, and in each of them, he fails the test of faith. K is trying to show what a crazy hard thing real faith is to attain.<br /><br />It's a difficult read. I have to take it in little pieces and occassionally throw in another book to keep myself sane. But if you're struggling with the concept of belief and devotion to something greater than yourself (something I've never figured out), it definitely raises useful questions.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3370006430957551260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1578425898" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5085531669181390523"><b>The Grand Leap of Faith</b><br/><br/><i>First published in The New Indian Express</i><br/><br/>I hope it is still in vogue among college-going folk to discuss not just the important matters of the day, but also vain philosophical question like, âWhat is the meaning of life?â Or: âIs there a God, and if there is a God, how are we to act?â<br/><br/>I remember discussing such things with friends in the wee hours of hostel rooftop parties. Although the arguments never resolved, they made us feel the need to be better prepared. Anyone who c</span>
  <span id="freeText5085531669181390523" style="display:none"><b>The Grand Leap of Faith</b><br /><br /><i>First published in The New Indian Express</i><br /><br />I hope it is still in vogue among college-going folk to discuss not just the important matters of the day, but also vain philosophical question like, âWhat is the meaning of life?â Or: âIs there a God, and if there is a God, how are we to act?â<br /><br />I remember discussing such things with friends in the wee hours of hostel rooftop parties. Although the arguments never resolved, they made us feel the need to be better prepared. Anyone who could quote Albert Camus, Jean-Paul Sartre, or any of the other famous philosopher-writers, usually had an edge. Thus, reading became a necessity to be able to prove a pointâat least the more objective of us felt so.<br /><br />Out of nostalgia or otherwise, some of the habits have persisted in me. With close friends, I still seek out opportunities to promote discussion on such quandaries. And I read philosophy to get a better understanding of how individuals more intelligent than me have thought of these questions.<br /><br />One of the names I have found myself quoting more frequently these days is SÃ¸ren Kierkegaard. <br /><br />Kierkegaard was a Danish theologian and philosopher in the 19th century, whom later philosophers labeled a âChristian existentialist.â This is to say that he pondered the same question that existentialists doâhuman conduct in the face of the meaninglessness of lifeâbut gave a Christian solution to it.<br /><br />Among his earliest works, the slim âFear and Tremblingâ is considered a masterpiece. In it, Kierkegaard complicates the Biblical story of Abraham, who was instructed by God to sacrifice his dear son, Isaac, atop a mountain. There is a three day journey between the instruction and the scheduled sacrifice. Kierkegaard brings our attention to this period, forcing us to consider Abrahamâs state of mind. What drove Abraham? What made him carry out an instruction that must have, at times, appeared harsh and absurd even to him?<br /><br />Kierkegaard posits that since Abraham fulfills an absolute duty to God, of obedience, the ethical paradigmâwhich makes killing Isaac a crimeâis suspended in his case. Note that this absolute duty isnât a transaction: Abraham isnât offered any virgins in paradise as compensation. For Kierkegaard, Abrahamâs faith is in fact a faith in the strength of the absurdâthat God, whose will is not to be questioned, will also somehow make things right. <br /><br />Kierkegaard thus considers faith a supremely difficult task. âFaithâ¦is not the immediate inclination of the heart but the paradox of existence.â To be faithful is to lead an absurd life and yet have the courage to grasp what is bound to appear an even larger absurdity: the idea of God. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5085531669181390523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3148742662" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3148742662" />
    <a title="Xander" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/60074558-xander"><img alt="Xander" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1490713726p2/60074558.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3148742662?book_show_action=true">Jan 19, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Xander" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Xander" href="/user/show/60074558-xander">Xander</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780140444490" href="/book/show/19742521-fear-and-trembling">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3148742662" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13591369610075606775">So far, 2020 hasn't been kind to me, as a reader. I have been disappointed by Michel Foucault's Madness and Civilization (1961), had to give up Bertrand Russell's The Analysis of Matter (1927) due to lack of background knowledge, and now Kierkegaard's Fear and Trembling (1843) hits me in the face. To be honest, I could have expected this. Being someone who values reason and rationality highly, and who is a convinced atheist, reading Kierkegaard is like looking for irritation. Yet, I also am a sk</span>
  <span id="freeText13591369610075606775" style="display:none">So far, 2020 hasn't been kind to me, as a reader. I have been disappointed by Michel Foucault's Madness and Civilization (1961), had to give up Bertrand Russell's The Analysis of Matter (1927) due to lack of background knowledge, and now Kierkegaard's Fear and Trembling (1843) hits me in the face. To be honest, I could have expected this. Being someone who values reason and rationality highly, and who is a convinced atheist, reading Kierkegaard is like looking for irritation. Yet, I also am a skeptic, and I wanted to put my own views on hold to find - perhaps - some gems of insight.<br /><br />Anyway, the book is written under a pseudonym, Johannes de Silentio - John the Silent. This name suggests a reluctant author, yet the (short) book is rather one long eulogy of how wonderful and admirable Abraham's cold-blooded decision to burn his own beloved son Isaac at the stake was. Why? Because it is an instance of blind faith, a height of human existence that even most heroes can't reach. <br /><br />To get there, you have to sacrifice reason. Reason, in its professional form called philosophy, pretends to uncover a rationally ordered world, and hence a rationally ordered life becomes possible. This is all delusion. In its fundamentals, the world and human existence are absurd - lacking any reason. This absurdity causes us anxiety. In Abraham's instance, reason would dictate to follow his feelings for his only and beloved son Isaac and in this instance of fear and trembling, find all kinds of solutions (again, through the use of reason) - like he misheard God's command, God doesn't exist, he dreamed, or accepting future punishment for his current misbehaviour towards God.<br /><br />Abraham doesn't do all that. In his stage of fear and trembling, he simpyl draws the sword - albeit in reluctance - to kill Isaac in order to burn him at the stake. It is in this moment, in this full surrender of Abraham to the irrationality and absurdity of life, that he is saved, and with him his beloved son. <br /><br />It is hard to know what Kierkegaard himself thought - seeing as he writes under a pseudonym - but I think we can glean some truth from his personal life. He was raised in a devout and pious (and rigid) environment by a christian father who was obsessed with his religion and depressed to his core. Kierkegaard didn't need to work since he inherited much wealth, and ended up studying theology. So we have here a man of leisure, being raised and trained to be a holy man. At the same time, he was a man of passion, a man who was obsessed with love, sex, and the asethetic life. Usually, this makes for a very bad and unhappy life - which was in fact what Kierkegaard got out of it. <br /><br />I think Fear and Trembling is a metaphor for this inner struggle. You want to do things, yet you know you ought to do other things. A tension arises, stress ensues. You have to stop these feelings of anguish, and reason cannot help you out of this situation. You have to act - to simply 'do'. And its in this 'acting' that you determine who you are. Kierkegaard's leap to irrationality, to escape all the tensions of the reasonable life, seems like a flight to me. Or rather, as a prison break from the dialectics of everyday life (especially for devout believers). <br /><br />And we should admire Abraham for his blind faith, his willingness to go through with the act he detested the most, simply because his faith reached such high passions and such a firm conviction. For Kierkegaard, in God's reward for Abraham, there is a lesson to be learned. It pays to be irrational and to embrace blind faith. Whether this kind of life is appropriate to most of us, has yet to be decided. Although the current Middle East seems to provide a decent counter-argument.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13591369610075606775" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15092059010738720867">I knew I was pretty deep in the philosophical pool -- again -- when concepts like "the teleological suspension of the ethical" started to float by, somewhat menacingly. This was also my first exposure to Kierkegaard, whose writing, though dense at times, was very compelling and often quite beautiful. <br/><br/>In this short volume, he wrestled with the Old Testament account of Abraham's journey to the mountain to sacrifice Isaac; he asks deep questions about Abraham's faith, and motives, and actions, all </span>
  <span id="freeText15092059010738720867" style="display:none">I knew I was pretty deep in the philosophical pool -- again -- when concepts like "the teleological suspension of the ethical" started to float by, somewhat menacingly. This was also my first exposure to Kierkegaard, whose writing, though dense at times, was very compelling and often quite beautiful. <br /><br />In this short volume, he wrestled with the Old Testament account of Abraham's journey to the mountain to sacrifice Isaac; he asks deep questions about Abraham's faith, and motives, and actions, all the while writing under the pseudonym Johannes de silentio. As noted in Alastair Hannay's introduction, "if we are to praise venerable figures like Abraham ... we should be made to appreciate what it was like to be Abraham undergoing the trial of faith".<br /><br />Indeed this little book will make you think deeply about spiritual truths and concepts, and is worth the effort invested -- even if you now and then feel like you're treading water.</span>

<div id="review_1713436704" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1713436704" />
    <a title="Khashayar Mohammadi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi"><img alt="Khashayar Mohammadi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493878334p2/32374363.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1713436704?book_show_action=true">Jul 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Khashayar Mohammadi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Khashayar Mohammadi" href="/user/show/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi">Khashayar Mohammadi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32374363-khashayar-mohammadi?shelf=scandinavian-lit">scandinavian-lit</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1713436704" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8273641435417632408">I have frequently read passages I've marked in the book, but it was the second time that I picked it up and read it cover to cover. I have heard countless times that Hegel, Kant or Derrida are hard to read, but I find Kierkegaard challenging on a whole other level. I find Kierkegaard to be one of the most profoundly artistic philosophers, especially when it comes to Faith and love. He is not just a great philosopher, but a fantastic writer, yet after numerous readings of Fear and Trembling, whic</span>
  <span id="freeText8273641435417632408" style="display:none">I have frequently read passages I've marked in the book, but it was the second time that I picked it up and read it cover to cover. I have heard countless times that Hegel, Kant or Derrida are hard to read, but I find Kierkegaard challenging on a whole other level. I find Kierkegaard to be one of the most profoundly artistic philosophers, especially when it comes to Faith and love. He is not just a great philosopher, but a fantastic writer, yet after numerous readings of Fear and Trembling, which by philosophical standards is rather short and succinct, I still feel there's more to harvest from it.<br /><br />Its a book that I won't be taking off my To-Read shelf any time soon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8273641435417632408" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
